  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e7ea64d5-0da8-469e-b226-6a7e3ee9be1d'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14280634345215233670">Set in the Ardennes Forest on Christmas Eve 1944, Sergeant Will Knott and five other GIs are ordered close to the German lines to establish an observation post in an abandoned chateau. Here they play at being soldiers in what seems to be complete isolation. That is, until the Germans begin revealing their whereabouts and leaving signs of their presence: a scarecrow, equipm</span>
  <span id="freeText14280634345215233670" style="display:none">Set in the Ardennes Forest on Christmas Eve 1944, Sergeant Will Knott and five other GIs are ordered close to the German lines to establish an observation post in an abandoned chateau. Here they play at being soldiers in what seems to be complete isolation. That is, until the Germans begin revealing their whereabouts and leaving signs of their presence: a scarecrow, equipment the squad had dropped on a retreat from a reconnaissance mission and, strangest of all, a small fir tree hung with fruit, candles, and cardboard stars. Suddenly, Knott and the others must unravel these mysteries, learning as they do about themselves, about one another, and about the "enemy," until <em>A Midnight Clear</em> reaches its unexpected climax, one of the most shattering in the literature of war.</span>

<div id="review_81866491" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81866491" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730330-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294641149p2/730330.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81866491?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/730330-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81866491" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7650464306274221704">Spend the Christmas season in the Ardennes Forest in a plundered, isolated chateau with the enemy paying strange (friendly?) visits while you try to escape the war. But for six bright young U.S. soldiers stationed in a lonely outpost to keep an eye on the Germans, World War II, seriously winding down in late 1944, must ultimately keep intruding.<br/><br/>1982's "A Midnight Clear" kept William Wharton, himself a World War II veteran, on a nice run after the excellent debut "Birdy" and "Dad." Like "Birdy," </span>
  <span id="freeText7650464306274221704" style="display:none">Spend the Christmas season in the Ardennes Forest in a plundered, isolated chateau with the enemy paying strange (friendly?) visits while you try to escape the war. But for six bright young U.S. soldiers stationed in a lonely outpost to keep an eye on the Germans, World War II, seriously winding down in late 1944, must ultimately keep intruding.<br /><br />1982's "A Midnight Clear" kept William Wharton, himself a World War II veteran, on a nice run after the excellent debut "Birdy" and "Dad." Like "Birdy," "A Midnight Clear" was made into a fine movie (Ethan Hawke, Gary Sinise, Peter Berg, etc.). The novel is simply but powerfully told by Will Knott, the leader by rank in a squad of six that runs itself. In the group is a man losing his grip after learning of the death of his child, a man who abandoned aspirations for the priesthood after deeming himself unworthy, an avenging Jew.<br /><br />Some German soldiers pay nighttime visits to the reluctant American soldiers, leaving as a calling card a scarecrow; telling the Americans, in German, to "sleep well;" engaging in a snowball fight. Is this all some sort of trap, or are the Germans really as uninterested in fighting this war as the Americans?<br /><br />Wharton probably doesn't probe the characters of these U.S. soldiers as deeply as he could have, and it seems as if Knott, as part of a group of soldiers with extremely high IQs, could have narrated in a more realistically eloquent manner. But these are quibbles. This is a novel whose quiet, simple power will stay with you.<br /><br />Knott's flashback narration of a confoundingly tender encounter several of the men had stateside with a woman who had just lost her husband to the war could nicely sum up the book itself, which gives us a wartime snapshot of people "passing through the mythical barrier between boys and men, men and death."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7650464306274221704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="EAK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1378538-eak"><img alt="EAK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217421049p2/1378538.jpg" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29930510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6094729792074426304">Another war story that I absolutely love. I read this in 8th grade for a book report, basically because it was on our bookshelves, and I was too lazy to go to the library to get something else. It was the first time I had read anything about WW II were the Germans weren't depicted as purely evil. Through the eyes of the teen-aged American soldier, Will, we see that much of the German military was made up of scared young boys and old men, far removed from the Nazi party.<br/>A movie based on the book </span>
  <span id="freeText6094729792074426304" style="display:none">Another war story that I absolutely love. I read this in 8th grade for a book report, basically because it was on our bookshelves, and I was too lazy to go to the library to get something else. It was the first time I had read anything about WW II were the Germans weren't depicted as purely evil. Through the eyes of the teen-aged American soldier, Will, we see that much of the German military was made up of scared young boys and old men, far removed from the Nazi party.<br />A movie based on the book came out around 1994 or so, and it is a beautiful adaptation, perfectly cast (Ethan Hawke as Will, Gary Sinise as Mother, etc.). Roger Ebert republished a review of it years later, saying it was an overlooked, fairy-tale like, gem. I agree.</span>

<div id="review_2589658059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2589658059" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2589658059?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Realini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Realini" href="/user/show/7873218-realini">Realini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2589658059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2785135539597332467"><br/>A Midnight Clear, based on the novel by William Wharton <br/>10 out of 10<br/><br/><br/><br/>Although not known as a classic in the vein of Saving Private Ryan, Dunkirk or some other fundamental war movie, A Midnight Clear is a formidable, extraordinary film, even if it does not have spectacular landings, fights between planes and the other paraphernalia of classic features of this kind.<br/><br/>Indeed, this stupendous work deals more, if not exclusively, with the psychological horrors of war, the derangement it causes in the mi</span>
  <span id="freeText2785135539597332467" style="display:none"><br />A Midnight Clear, based on the novel by William Wharton <br />10 out of 10<br /><br /><br /><br />Although not known as a classic in the vein of Saving Private Ryan, Dunkirk or some other fundamental war movie, A Midnight Clear is a formidable, extraordinary film, even if it does not have spectacular landings, fights between planes and the other paraphernalia of classic features of this kind.<br /><br />Indeed, this stupendous work deals more, if not exclusively, with the psychological horrors of war, the derangement it causes in the minds of people and the impact it has on group of American and German fighters.<br />It is World War II, near Christmas, in 1944, when the Nazi forces have been pushed back, but there is still much fighting to be done.<br /><br />The cast is phenomenal:<br />Peter Berg, Kevin Dillon, Frank Whaley and especially Ethan Hawke as Seargent Will Knott and Gary Sinise in the role of the fragile Vance Wilkins, also called Mother.<br />This group of young Americans is sent into the forest, to secure and hold on to a house that might be crucial in an upcoming battle.<br /><br />They are supposed to inform their base on any movements and they do that up to a point.<br />As soldiers keep guard, they start hearing noises and seem to see movements.<br /><br />There is even a :<br /><br />Good night, said in German...I guess<br /><br />With time, they see indeed that there are Germans in the area, but regardless of the fact that they are scared to death when they first meet each other, nothing happens!<br />Furthermore, the couple of men that are on duty out in the snow see that they are attacked...<br /><br />Only with...snowballs!<br /><br />Gradually, they start communicating with each other and "Father" is able to translate.<br />The Germans have had enough of the war.<br /><br />They propose some meetings, which are somewhat awkward for they are understandably suspicious of each other...they had been at a vicious war for long enough to see many dead and worry about a trap or an ambush.<br />A further complication on the American side is the need to present a superior officer, to confer seriousness, gravitas to negotiations.<br /><br />For the Germans have decided: they want to stop fighting, surrender...well not to a group of five or seven ordinary soldiers, but in the end it looks like they may pull off this great stunt.<br />There are some clouds to consider, first one is to avoid the implication of superiors in all this, or at least not until it is all fixed and done.<br /><br />This we can see is more than sensible, especially when an incredibly pompous, ridiculous, absurd, self absorbed, vicious, malevolent bastard of an officer would show up.<br />He is more than revolting!<br /><br />The other major obstacle is "Mother".<br />Ever since tragedy has struck in his family back at home, this man has lost his grip.<br /><br />Hence, they do not inform him of the plan to take the Germans prisoners, even if later on they would describe the tragedy that would ensue as the moment when he saved all that could be and he acted as a hero.<br />His comrades would actually propose that this suffering manger the Medal of Honor or some other prestigious Army decoration.<br /><br />What happens at the culminating point, in the climax, which is also a nadir of the film in terms of loss of life, will not be mentioned, for reasons of spoiler alert - someone criticized me a few years ago and I try to spoil it as little as I can...when there is no I resistible urge to say all that happens.<br />Suffice it to say that it is an event that gives great weight to the drama.<br /><br />A Midnight Clear is adept, insightful, though provoking, superior, excellent, memorable motion picture.<br />And one of the best on the subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2785135539597332467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17633114010114536819">A story of a small group of WWII soldiers in the Ardennes in December of 1944. They have an encounter with some German soldiers who seem as tired of the war as they are. The situation remains, as their war experiences have been throughout, FUBAR.<br/><br/>This is a small story; you won't gain any new knowledge of the bigger war picture, but it is a very moving story of a group of young men trying to get through a horrific time to make it out to the other side of their lives. Highly recommended.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2309874900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2309874900" />
    <a title="Will Chin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21982709-will-chin"><img alt="Will Chin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430313775p2/21982709.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2309874900?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Chin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Chin" href="/user/show/21982709-will-chin">Will Chin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21982709-will-chin?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309874900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4195807622391364980">Full disclosure: I read this book while being slightly under the weather. I caught something during a trip to Indonesia and, while it wasn't anything serious, it did affect the way I read this book. If I had read this book with a clearer mind, perhaps I'd have rated it higher. Nonetheless, you don't choose when you get to fall sick so, sorry Wharton. <br/><br/>Anyway, I will tell you what I like about A Midnight Clear. Having been through the military, Wharton does a great job at recreating what it is lik</span>
  <span id="freeText4195807622391364980" style="display:none">Full disclosure: I read this book while being slightly under the weather. I caught something during a trip to Indonesia and, while it wasn't anything serious, it did affect the way I read this book. If I had read this book with a clearer mind, perhaps I'd have rated it higher. Nonetheless, you don't choose when you get to fall sick so, sorry Wharton. <br /><br />Anyway, I will tell you what I like about A Midnight Clear. Having been through the military, Wharton does a great job at recreating what it is like out in the field. The banter between the soldiers are perfect, and the things we do to entertain ourselves in between actions is true to life as well. There is a line somewhere in the book about how the war is mostly just soldiers sitting around and waiting for things to happen. I've never been to war before, but military training is more or less the same. You sit around and wait for something to happen. There was a real sense of familiarity while I was reading the book. <br /><br />However, as true as it is that the military is most a bunch of waiting around, it doesn't translate quite well to the book. There are entire sections of this book where literally nothing happens, and you are stuck in the wintry landscape with our characters, just waiting for something — anything — to happen. As realistic as that might be, it doesn't make for a particularly good read. It also doesn't help that all six characters have nicknames, and the writer uses nicknames and real names interchangeably. It's bad enough that I was reading this while being a little high on medicine, it's worse that I had to figure out who's who on the page. I admit that it got a bit confusing for me. <br /><br />But I think the core of the book is good. It is about how wars are mostly fought by people at the top, with us lowly soldiers as dispensable pawns. Most of us don't actually want to fight. Most of us just want to not die, and it doesn't matter which side of the battle lines you are on. I think there is a tragedy in this book that is expertly masked by the tragic-comedy approach by the writer, and that's a brilliant way to drive the message home. I just wished that I wasn't ill while reading this book, and that the middle sections of the book didn't feel so bogged down by endless descriptions of sentry shifts and bridge games. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4195807622391364980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer2782484580369472789">I’ve passed up on seeing the movie version of this book several times because I really don’t like Ethan Hawke. Way back when TLC and A&E were actually watchable there was a show call The Great Books. One episode was about Great Expectations. Hawke was interviewed because a movie version with him was coming out. Whatever he said convinced me that he really didn’t understand what he read. Combine that with I don’t think he can act, and well, there you go. <br/> <br/>	But I just might watch it now.<br/><br/>	Wharton’s</span>
  <span id="freeText2782484580369472789" style="display:none">I’ve passed up on seeing the movie version of this book several times because I really don’t like Ethan Hawke. Way back when TLC and A&amp;E were actually watchable there was a show call The Great Books. One episode was about Great Expectations. Hawke was interviewed because a movie version with him was coming out. Whatever he said convinced me that he really didn’t understand what he read. Combine that with I don’t think he can act, and well, there you go. <br /> <br />	But I just might watch it now.<br /><br />	Wharton’s novel is All Quiet on the Western Front set in World War II with a dash of Catch-22. It is told from the viewpoint of a young sergeant who finds himself (and his men) in a rather confusing situation with some Germans. It’s true that some of the supporting characters could be more developed. The story, however, does grip the read, and the voice is compelling. You know how it’s going to end, you have to, but the book still moves you. It always will. <br />	There’s something to be said for that.<br /><br />	It isn’t all depressing; there are some wonderful funny passages and scenes. It’s not about heroism, but about people struggling to be people. It is really good. Wonderful.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16188302540903232370">Picked this up as a recommendation in a reading challenge.<br/><br/>The writing is top-notch and the setup is phenomenal. I grew to like the charactes and didn't want anything bad to happen to them, but I kind of sensed the ending was going to be ... wrenching? That's not a spoiler. This is a WWII novel. So it took me about a week to finally sit down and read the last seventy pages.<br/><br/>The ending is actually the only re</span>
  <span id="freeText16188302540903232370" style="display:none">Picked this up as a recommendation in a reading challenge.<br /><br />The writing is top-notch and the setup is phenomenal. I grew to like the charactes and didn't want anything bad to happen to them, but I kind of sensed the ending was going to be ... wrenching? That's not a spoiler. This is a WWII novel. You didn't expect it to end with everyone linking hands and singing Happy Birthday to You. So it took me about a week to finally sit down and read the last seventy pages.<br /><br />The ending is actually the only reason I didn't give it five stars, because while wrenching, it also fell apart and didn't flow from the beginning. After the dark part where everything went haywire, it should have ended--except there was this long, twisting denoument that I found confusing. But overall, it was a good book and I'm glad to have read it.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78739684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14534979502327588419">William Wharton brings the reader into the trenches with a small group of teenage and twenty year old soldiers in world war II at Christmas time. They run into a small group of German soldiers who want to communicate and ultimately want to surrender to the US after a few nights of hesitant snowball fights and Christmas carols. Wharton shows you the horror of war and the humanity of the boys (on both sides) fighting it. <br/><br/>Especially timely with my nephew in Afghanistan right now...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_61365490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61365490" />
    <a title="Wendy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2463977-wendy"><img alt="Wendy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596165163p2/2463977.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61365490?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wendy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wendy" href="/user/show/2463977-wendy">Wendy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=vcfa-reading">vcfa-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=literary-war-fiction">literary-war-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2463977-wendy?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61365490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15880343795950717345">I first read this book many years ago, in seventh grade during one of my earliest forays into war fiction. I must have affected me deeply because a few vivid scenes and images still stand out in my brain over 15 years later: a snowball fight between young German and American soldiers isolated in a forest (the Germans stuck sticks into their own snowballs to mimic the shape of their grenades), red crosses painted with blood to mimic medical personnel, frozen bodies "dancing" with one another, and</span>
  <span id="freeText15880343795950717345" style="display:none">I first read this book many years ago, in seventh grade during one of my earliest forays into war fiction. I must have affected me deeply because a few vivid scenes and images still stand out in my brain over 15 years later: a snowball fight between young German and American soldiers isolated in a forest (the Germans stuck sticks into their own snowballs to mimic the shape of their grenades), red crosses painted with blood to mimic medical personnel, frozen bodies "dancing" with one another, and the narrator's "legendary" case of the runs. I finally read it again, and I'm so glad that I did.<br /><br /><i>A Midnight Clear</i> tells the (purportedly sorta true) story of a depleted squad of intelligence &amp; reconnaissance specialists who are sent to man and monitor a forward location at an isolated chateau at the start of the Battle of the Bulge. Narrator Will Knott (aka Wont) informs us that these guys are insanely smart, had all received special training and then were transferred to the front as cannon fodder thanks to a bureaucratic error. Wont's own chatty yet introspective style seems to prove his intellectual prowess. Normally I like cleverness, but my biggest issue with this book is that there is a little too much of it--the endless bridge hands and trivia that fill the soldier's many hours of boredom force the pace to a crawl. Also, the six American GIs all talk with the same witty, smart-ass soldier tone as Won't himself, and it's impossible to tell them apart without dialogue tags. Even by the end, the reader only knows most of these characters by the stereotypes they represent: the NY Jew, the almost-pastor, the sensitive weakling, the mechanic. Despite this, the situation of their isolation, the creeping terror they experience cut off from anything familiar, makes it impossible not to identify and empathize. Soon they make accidental contact with a similarly isolated, strangely submissive group of German soldiers who call "Schlaf gut, Ami!" (sleep well, Yank!) each night across the clearing. This isn't a black &amp; white war story where good fights evil, but it's still a gripping, sometimes terrifying, always thought-provoking yarn.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15880343795950717345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15611372714169383541" style="display:none">"A Midnight Clear" is a modest inquiry into war, youth and extinction. <div id="review_3528641452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3528641452" />
    <a title="Steven Haywood Yaskell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16068436-steven-haywood-yaskell"><img alt="Steven Haywood Yaskell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357277559p2/16068436.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3528641452?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Haywood Yaskell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Haywood Yaskell" href="/user/show/16068436-steven-haywood-yaskell">Steven Haywood Yaskell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3528641452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14906771462997752144">The professional American writers of the period in which William Wharton (Albert Du Aime) lived for the most part were poetasters to the fine art of combat - Mailer and Jones being principle and very visible, muscular, Hemingway-esque exceptions. Du Aime was not a professional war novelist in this competition, but he had credentials to the act of actually being a shooting contestant in the non-literary fight between the major literary gladiators, when, in his later years, he wrote this magnifice</span>
  <span id="freeText14906771462997752144" style="display:none">The professional American writers of the period in which William Wharton (Albert Du Aime) lived for the most part were poetasters to the fine art of combat - Mailer and Jones being principle and very visible, muscular, Hemingway-esque exceptions. Du Aime was not a professional war novelist in this competition, but he had credentials to the act of actually being a shooting contestant in the non-literary fight between the major literary gladiators, when, in his later years, he wrote this magnificent tale of his wartime fight. Du Aime was not a major gladiator in this literary contest - the "best war novel," begun in America inadvertently by Stephen Crane and ending, intentionally, with Hemingway - who tried, but failed (not to deplore Hemingway's literary and especially linguistic talent and innovation to the art). But in the long run, Du Aime may just have equaled Crane, here. Most of all, he was a near-innocent dragged by the needs of the service &amp; typical military and bureaucratic incompetence, into a savage fight. He was relegated to this position by more than a little cowardice on behalf of his fellow citizens, and it is shown in technicolor in this work. <br /><br />It is for this reason we should be very grateful for the errors that lead such a person into an actual fight of the magnitude in which - like Erich Maria Remarque - Du Aime witnessed, fought, and survived. (Note: the background tone of this book is very much like All Quiet On The Western Front.) We have a simple, intelligent, kind soul who - due to the vagaries of changed promises in the middle of a brutal, socialist/royalist-inspired war - wound up briefly as a "dog-faced soldier" and, even if he was inherently not one, was at least able to write their story from a position so close he could not miss. Du Aime's journey in WWII was not so very unlike Remarque's, and, as kindred spirits, the naivete of the American drawn into a war by Germans, forces the same human story as Remarque's out of this American author. The experiences tie the two men so closely together as authors and as men, it heals the wounds of two world wars in these pages in a most nuanced and beautiful, humane manner.<br /><br />Promised college training and being saved for post-war college-level reconstruction work in applied sciences and as history recorders, etc., by Henry Stimson, the blithering incompetence that is endemic to the American civilian-led-by-professional (read: wealthy) cadre of West Point/Annapolis officers devolved into the "needs of the service" by the main procurer of personnel and materiel, General McNair. In the end, as the magic ball is passed, many of these "ASTPRs" get handed private stripes and guns, instead of officer's commissions and slide rules. It is a great thing that such things occur rarely in America: for the socialist/royalist (both &amp; the same) forces of eternal warfare had to be blunted somewhere. WWII's end meant the end of royalty and war-as-policy for achieving political ends forever. In a way, the tremors of this war still reverberate in the form of academic socialists and blunted royalists banging at the US's door in the form of European "Union" and UN tyranny. But, this will ebb away.<br /><br />In this novel, we see Henry Fleming's final lesson: that massed war leads to mass destruction so profound, the inner mind is shown as equally shattered as the surroundings in which the person walks. <br /><br />No puke-filled "anti-war" drama label here to suit the politics of the American Writer's Union. With A Midnight Clear, we have explored for us not just the utter stupidity of war, but its inevitability - couched in a clear, beautiful warning: NO MORE. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14906771462997752144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3094101114" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3094101114" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70058633-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3094101114?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/70058633-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21086998-a-midnight-clear">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70058633-rebecca?shelf=us">us</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70058633-rebecca?shelf=belgium">belgium</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3094101114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12840983239455986154">I’m surprised that I had never heard of this until a few weeks ago, especially as there is a film starring Ethan Hawke. This is the story of six (mainly young) American men, part of a reconnaissance group stationed in a chateau in the Ardennes forest at the time of the Battle of the Bulge. Personally I preferred the first half of the book, before we really know what is happening, when there is a series of eerie nocturnal events that suggest there is a similar group of German soldiers also statio</span>
  <span id="freeText12840983239455986154" style="display:none">I’m surprised that I had never heard of this until a few weeks ago, especially as there is a film starring Ethan Hawke. This is the story of six (mainly young) American men, part of a reconnaissance group stationed in a chateau in the Ardennes forest at the time of the Battle of the Bulge. Personally I preferred the first half of the book, before we really know what is happening, when there is a series of eerie nocturnal events that suggest there is a similar group of German soldiers also stationed nearby. The tense atmosphere created, as well as the confusion and overall weirdness of the situation was brilliantly done. <br /><br />I liked it less as things became more ‘real’, but that shouldn’t really detract from the actual book (I think I had just written a different book in my own head ahead of time!). Although there are elements that feel very much like a fairytale - the forest setting, the snowy weather, the isolation, the magical Christmas elements - ultimately this becomes a story about the futility of war and the youth, inexperience, fear and confusion of most of its combatants. It can also on occasion be dull, as the group sit around finding things to do and talk about and with the relentless cycle of guard duties, but, as the author writes, this is what much of wartime really is.<br /><br />I don’t read much war literature so this gave me some very different perspectives - the boredom, the confusion, the dirtiness. Overall incredibly moving and sad. Definitely a different and poignant festive read that will stay with me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12840983239455986154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Grant Masson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7249290-grant-masson"><img alt="Grant Masson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482797687p2/7249290.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2701228170?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Grant Masson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Grant Masson" href="/user/show/7249290-grant-masson">Grant Masson</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2701228170" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2396111807105111718">The book tells the story of six young American soldiers fighting for their lives during the end of the Second World War. Sent as a reconnaissance troop at the back of the front line in Ardennes, they encounter Germans, also lost in the war chaos. All characters stay in front of a dramatic dilemma – they are fed up with killing, but at the same time they don’t want to die. Both sides decide to help each other. Their meeting takes place in a moonlit night…<br/><br/>The plot of this book focuses on showing f</span>
  <span id="freeText2396111807105111718" style="display:none">The book tells the story of six young American soldiers fighting for their lives during the end of the Second World War. Sent as a reconnaissance troop at the back of the front line in Ardennes, they encounter Germans, also lost in the war chaos. All characters stay in front of a dramatic dilemma – they are fed up with killing, but at the same time they don’t want to die. Both sides decide to help each other. Their meeting takes place in a moonlit night…<br /><br />The plot of this book focuses on showing fear and humiliation of people, who are not prepared to face the realities of war. It makes us realise the chaos and absurd of the situation in which they found themselves. Living in fear and constant uncertainty of tomorrow causes that after years of fighting they become nervous wrecks. And the shocking ending provokes us to think how fragile human life is.<br /><br />‘A Midnight Clear’ is well written, albeit very simple language. Naturalistic narration is mixed with humour, emotion and surprise.<br /><br />I would really recommend reading Wharton’s novel, since it is an in-depth study of human psyche in extreme situations and a classic of its kind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2396111807105111718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2072107551984564132">I enjoyed this.<br/><br/>Wharton writes like he's sat there telling you a story: "I've got mud all over my boots and I'm slipping in the snow as I try to get to the foxhole" - that kind of thing. I like that approach.<br/>The story is a sad one, it's a short journey for the reader from "Band of Brothers"-style opening in the Ardennes, through an almost fairy tale period of snow-covered woodland and a ruined chateau, to a shocking conclusion which leaves the reader thinking "yeah, I can imagine how that's just </span>
  <span id="freeText2072107551984564132" style="display:none">I enjoyed this.<br /><br />Wharton writes like he's sat there telling you a story: "I've got mud all over my boots and I'm slipping in the snow as I try to get to the foxhole" - that kind of thing. I like that approach.<br />The story is a sad one, it's a short journey for the reader from "Band of Brothers"-style opening in the Ardennes, through an almost fairy tale period of snow-covered woodland and a ruined chateau, to a shocking conclusion which leaves the reader thinking "yeah, I can imagine how that's just what would happen". It's about young, inexperienced men forced into situations they haven't been alive long enough to deal with, yet having to live with the consequences of their inability to process events for the rest of their lives.<br /><br />It's a story which leads you to one of two conclusions: 1. war is universally bad, stupid and pointless, or 2. soldiers really ought to be old men who move slowly and with purpose.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1840073442256417385">3rd January 1999<br/>William Wharton<br/>A Midnight Clear<br/><br/>I read this in the autumn, about the same time as Robert Greaves “Goodbye to all that”.<br/><br/>Two wars, two intelligent young men sent out as cannon fodder.   <span id="freeText1840073442256417385" style="display:none">3rd January 1999<br />William Wharton<br />A Midnight Clear<br /><br />I read this in the autumn, about the same time as Robert Greaves “Goodbye to all that”.<br /><br />Two wars, two intelligent young men sent out as cannon fodder. Greaves, the British upper middle class in the First World War and Wharton the American genius in the Second World War.<br />Wharton tells a hard story with a romantic ending in a lighthearted American way. Greaves was bitter when he wrote his book, much more aware and much less willing to accept the waste of life caused by the incompetent generals and the political imperatives.<br />Wharton pokes fun at the generals, and his characters crack up and go mad. But there is always a lighthearted flippancy in his approach, no heartfelt bitterness. “Have a nice day” even in war.<br /></span>

<div id="review_2726427997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2726427997" />
    <a title="Lori Adams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7034477-lori-adams"><img alt="Lori Adams" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3147872031592032246">A well written book. The reader is transported to the Ardennes forest during WWII. A small group of soldiers are sent to a deserted chateau to do reconnaissance. They encounter a small group of German soldiers. It is nearing Christmas and unlikely things begin to happen between the two enemies. Small surprises. Wheaton’s descriptive writing takes you into a beautiful chateau set in what seems like it must be a beautiful forest. The chateau is filled with beautiful objects hidden in the attic tha</span>
  <span id="freeText3147872031592032246" style="display:none">A well written book. The reader is transported to the Ardennes forest during WWII. A small group of soldiers are sent to a deserted chateau to do reconnaissance. They encounter a small group of German soldiers. It is nearing Christmas and unlikely things begin to happen between the two enemies. Small surprises. Wheaton’s descriptive writing takes you into a beautiful chateau set in what seems like it must be a beautiful forest. The chateau is filled with beautiful objects hidden in the attic that are appreciated but...... <br />It is a good book but is basically another sad story about the war. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3147872031592032246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_696116376" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/696116376" />
    <a title="Sherie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1270607-sherie"><img alt="Sherie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249929073p2/1270607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/696116376?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherie" href="/user/show/1270607-sherie">Sherie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer696116376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11034860694336958126">The author knows the horror of war as well as how to put his readers into the moment. I was at once in Germany in 1944, enduring the teeth rattling cold, all the while fearing that each breath could easily be my last. Will Knott finds himself with the rank of sergeant by virtue of still being alive and is sent with what remains of his unit to the bowels of the forest to check on enemy movement . The author makes sure we know all the main characters as they try to puzzle out why they find themsel</span>
  <span id="freeText11034860694336958126" style="display:none">The author knows the horror of war as well as how to put his readers into the moment. I was at once in Germany in 1944, enduring the teeth rattling cold, all the while fearing that each breath could easily be my last. Will Knott finds himself with the rank of sergeant by virtue of still being alive and is sent with what remains of his unit to the bowels of the forest to check on enemy movement . The author makes sure we know all the main characters as they try to puzzle out why they find themselves in this position. The enemy makes his presence known, but in a peculiar way. As Will and his friends try to make sense of what is happening, the senselessness of war prevails and we are left to wonder just who is the enemy after all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11034860694336958126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15781538811969442342">Hemmingway's For Whom the Bell Tolls, and A Farewell to Arms will forever be regarded classic modern American literature. And then there is William Wharton's remarkable war tale, A Midnight Clear, which will never climb the lofty ranks of its literary brethren. Personally, I found this book much better than Hemmingway's novels. I found Wharton's prose more profound and compelling enough to write down simply because I loved the way the sentences looked on paper. I've discovered, to my surprise Wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15781538811969442342" style="display:none">Hemmingway's For Whom the Bell Tolls, and A Farewell to Arms will forever be regarded classic modern American literature. And then there is William Wharton's remarkable war tale, A Midnight Clear, which will never climb the lofty ranks of its literary brethren. Personally, I found this book much better than Hemmingway's novels. I found Wharton's prose more profound and compelling enough to write down simply because I loved the way the sentences looked on paper. I've discovered, to my surprise Wharton also wrote Birdy. Birdy was a great movie. I look forward to reading the book. </span>

<div id="review_426957642" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/426957642" />
    <a title="Ross McCoubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12747753-ross-mccoubrey"><img alt="Ross McCoubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349130387p2/12747753.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ross McCoubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ross McCoubrey" href="/user/show/12747753-ross-mccoubrey">Ross McCoubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer426957642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10453606899456093802">This is a terrific story, not just for fans of war-time fiction. My only problem with it is the use of the First Person Present Tense which I always find very hard to relate to as it can not be happening at the moment I am reading it, as it is documented. That aside, this is an amazing work and, if you can focus on the story and not the way it is told, is a very rewarding experience.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZBQJ5d1J79Wl9nJ0qV11ZZfrSTD/gZnT2j8iPz7lwVkOCdCWMA0ifVdiUxr1G0VhptxCSGyCx3HNNUuSfTZIdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('q5U+EI1gi4ljur79lgvx+Eo9MgasVcgFLN5T4/teBPrBiOdjYCRGIZEun5PKTcH8ewo5fj9Wlqc71DpOuI2N1w==')})
  }

Обсадата на Сараево е започнала преди година и краят не се вижда, също както на братоубийствената война между довчерашните съюзни югославски народи. Двойка млади сърби стоят в кухнята си, потънали в мълчание. Давор, хрис<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5946606604301131487\" style=\"display:none\">Топла майска нощ през 1993 г. Обсадата на Сараево е започнала преди година и краят не се вижда, също както на братоубийствената война между довчерашните съюзни югославски народи. Двойка млади сърби стоят в кухнята си, потънали в мълчание. Давор, християнин, и Айда, мюсюлманка, са съхранили любовта си сред разрухата и безумието, които царят в обсадения град. Знаят, че наближава поредното кратко, едва половинчасово затишие, когато снайперистите почиват. Загледани в стрелките на часовника, те са взели решението да избягат – да достигнат свободата… или да посрещнат смъртта.\nДвадесет години по-късно българин пътува към Сараево, за да се срещне със сръбския преводач на книгата си. Скоро се озовават в кръчма и неусетно заговарят за войната. С напредването на часовете празните бутилки на масата се увеличават, а отдавна погребани тайни излизат на повърхността.\nДве нощи. Четири съдби. Всички водещи до най-важните въпроси.\nКой запали тази война? Dreaming no farther than Czechoslovakia, the young reporter found himself sent to India. Wide-eyed and captivated, he would discover in those days his life’s work—to understand and describe the world in its remotest reaches, in all its multiplicity. From the rituals of sunrise at Persepolis to the incongruity of Louis Armstrong performing before a stone-faced crowd in Khartoum, Kapuscinski gives us the non-Western world as he first saw it, through still-virginal Western eyes.The companion on his travels: a volume of Herodotus, a gift from his first boss. Whether in China, Poland, Iran, or the Congo, it was the “father of history”—and, as Kapuscinski would realize, of globalism—who helped the young correspondent to make sense of events, to find the story where it did not obviously exist. It is this great forerunner’s spirit—both supremely worldly and innately Occidental—that would continue to whet Kapuscinski’s ravenous appetite for discovering the broader world and that has made him our own indispensable companion on any leg of that perpetual journey.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17270367051851373352\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n When the narrator moves into the area, she discovers everyone--and everything--has a story. With the help of Marta, her enigmatic neighbor, the narrator accumulates these stories, tracing the history of Nowa Ruda from the its founding to the lives of its saints, from the caller who wins the radio quiz every day to the man who causes international tension when he dies straddling the border between Poland and Czechoslovakia.\n\nEach of the stories represents a brick and they interlock to reveal the immense monument that is the town. What emerges is the message that the history of any place--no matter how humble--is limitless, that by describing or digging at the roots of a life, a house, or a neighborhood, one can see all the connections, not only with one\'s self and one\'s dreams but also with all of the universe.\n\nRichly imagined, weaving anecdote with recipes and gossip, Tokarczuk\'s novel is an epic of a small place. Since its publication in 1998 it has remained a bestseller in Poland.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/157249.William_Wharton">About William Wharton</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/157249.William_Wharton"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1226622761p3/157249.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/157249.William_Wharton">William Wharton
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
204
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2912086058194901336">William Wharton (7 November 1925 - 29 October 2008), the pen name of the author Albert William Du Aime (pronounced as doo-EM), was an American-born author best known for his first novel Birdy, which was also successful as a film.<br/><br/>Wharton was born in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania. He graduated from Upper Darby High School in 1943, and was inducted into the school's Wall of Fame in 1997. He volunteered </span>
  <span id="freeText2912086058194901336" style="display:none">William Wharton (7 November 1925 - 29 October 2008), the pen name of the author Albert William Du Aime (pronounced as doo-EM), was an American-born author best known for his first novel Birdy, which was also successful as a film.<br /><br />Wharton was born in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania. He graduated from Upper Darby High School in 1943, and was inducted into the school's Wall of Fame in 1997. He volunteered to serve in the United States Army during World War II, and was assigned to serve in a unit to be trained as engineers. He ended up being assigned to serve in the infantry and was severely wounded in the Battle of the Bulge. After his discharge, he attended the University of California, Los Angeles and received a undergraduate degree in art and a doctorate in psychology, later teaching art in the Los Angeles Unified School District.<br /><br />His first novel Birdy was published in 1978 when he was more than 50 years old. Birdy was a critical and popular success, and Alan Parker directed a film version starring Nicolas Cage and Matthew Modine. After the publication of Birdy and through the early 1990s, Du Aime published eight novels, including Dad and A Midnight Clear, both of which were also filmed, the former starring Jack Lemmon.<br /><br />Many of the protagonists of Wharton's novels, despite having different names and backgrounds, have similar experiences, attitudes, and traits that lead one to presume that they are partly autobiographical[citation needed]. There is precious little certifiable biography available about Wharton / Du Aime. He served in France and Germany in World War II in the 87th Infantry Division, was a painter, spent part of his adult life living on a houseboat as an artist in France, raised several children (not all of whom appreciated his philosophy of child-rearing), is a reasonably skilled carpenter and handyman, and has suffered from profound gastrointestinal problems.<br /><br />In 1988, Wharton's daughter, Kate; his son-in-law, Bert; and their two children, two-year-old Dayiel and eight-month-old Mia, were killed in a horrific 23-car motor vehicle accident near Albany, Oregon, that was caused by the smoke generated by grass-burning on nearby farmland. In 1995, Wharton wrote a (mostly) non-fiction book, Ever After: A Father's True Story, in which he recounts the incidents leading up to the accident, his family's subsequent grief, and the three years he devoted to pursuing redress in the Oregon court system for the field-burning that caused the accident. Houseboat on Seine, a memoir, was published in 1996, about Wharton's purchase and renovation of a houseboat.<br /><br />It is worth to be noted that he gained an enormous and very hard to be explained popularity in Poland, which was followed by many editions as well as meetings and, eventually, some works prepared and edited only in Polish.<br /><br />Wharton died on October 29, 2008 of an infection he contracted while being hospitalized for blood-pressure problems.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2912086058194901336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
